ehdk2mb0"><h1 class="css-1j5ig2m e1h9rw200" id="link-7010029e" itemprop="headline"><span>Trump Administration Officials at Odds Over C.I.A.’s Role in Afghanistan</span></h1></div><div class="css-6n7j50"><button aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="More sharing options" class="css-16ogagc" data-testid=""><svg class="css-uhuo44" height="13" viewbox="0 0 16 13" width="16"><path clip-rule="evenodd" d="M15.406 5.359L8.978 0v3.215C3.82 3.215.406 8.107.406 12.66 1.653 9.133 4.29 7.517 8.978 7.517v3.2l6.428-5.358z" fill="#000" fill-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></button></div></li><li class="css-60hakz"></li><li class="css-l72opv"></li></ul></div></div></li></ul></div></div></header><section class="meteredContent css-1i2y565" itemprop="articleBody" name="articleBody"><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">WASHINGTON — Senior White House advisers have proposed secretly expanding the C.I.A.’s presence in Afghanistan if international forces begin to withdraw from the country, according to American officials. But C.I.A. and military officials have expressed reservations, prompting a debate in the administration that could complicate negotiations with the Taliban to end the war.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Some administration officials want C.I.A.-backed militia forces in Afghanistan to serve as part of a counterterrorism force that would prevent the resurgence of the Islamic State or Al Qaeda as American military troops prepare to leave — in effect, an insurance policy.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But others are skeptical that the shadowy militias, many of which face accusations of brutality, can serve as a bulwark against terrorism without the support of the American military.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The C.I.A. director, Gina Haspel, has raised logistical concerns about the plan with other administration officials, emphasizing that the agency operatives — who marshal the militias to hunt Taliban, Qaeda and Islamic State militants — largely depend on the military for airstrikes, overhead surveillance, medical support and bomb technicians.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Skeptics have also noted that American intelligence agencies do not believe the Islamic State’s presence in Afghanistan justifies a vast increase in resources given limited budgets. The Islamic State’s affiliate there is <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/08/02/world/middleeast/isis-afghanistan-us-military.html?module=inline" title="">not an immediate threat to the West,</a> despite its regular attacks on Afghan civilians and continuing fight with the Taliban, according to intelligence officials.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The disagreement about the future of the C.I.A. in Afghanistan underscores the fault lines within the administration between those who want a final withdrawal and those who fear it would expose the United States to terrorist threats. This article is based on interviews with a half-dozen current or former officials briefed on the administration’s discussions. The C.I.A. declined to comment, and the White House declined to respond on the record to a request for comment.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The issue could pose an obstacle as American and Taliban negotiators seek a deal to end the longest war in United States history. The Taliban have made clear that they see little difference between American military troops and C.I.A. officers, and they have insisted in the current peace talks in Qatar that the C.I.A. must leave along with international military forces in the coming months or over the next few years.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militias3/merlin_150651867_c1dc0239-2c74-41a6-a73f-16bb18165ece-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><img alt="American military advisers and Afghan soldiers last year in Maidan Wardak Province. The Taliban have insisted in the current peace talks that the C.I.A. must leave Afghanistan along with international military forces." class="css-1m50asq" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militias3/merlin_150651867_c1dc0239-2c74-41a6-a73f-16bb18165ece-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" itemprop="url" sizes="((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 60vw, 100vw" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militias3/merlin_150651867_c1dc0239-2c74-41a6-a73f-16bb18165ece-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" srcset="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militias3/merlin_150651867_c1dc0239-2c74-41a6-a73f-16bb18165ece-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militias3/merlin_150651867_c1dc0239-2c74-41a6-a73f-16bb18165ece-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militias3/merlin_150651867_c1dc0239-2c74-41a6-a73f-16bb18165ece-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w"/></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">American military advisers and Afghan soldiers last year in Maidan Wardak Province. The Taliban have insisted in the current peace talks that the C.I.A. must leave Afghanistan along with international military forces.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>James Mackenzie/Reuters</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The top American negotiator, Zalmay Khalilzad, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/US4AfghanPeace/status/1167945304290672642?s=20" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">said over the weekend</a> that the two sides were on “the threshold of an agreement” after the latest round of negotiations. They have broadly covered the fate of the Afghan security forces but have not dealt directly with the militia groups, or American support for them, said a person familiar with the negotiations.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Afghan government is not part of the negotiations, but the deal is expected to open a path for talks between the government and the Taliban.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Supporters of the plan to expand C.I.A. support for the militias believe it could address the most potent critique of the peace talks: that a withdrawal of American forces would leave the United States with little ability to prevent terrorist groups from once again using Afghanistan as a base of operations.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“The high-end forces, including C.I.A.-supported forces, are not going to win any war for you, but they may degrade the capability of terrorist groups,” said Seth G. Jones, a scholar at the Center for Strategic and International Studies and a former adviser to the commanding general of American Special Operations forces in Afghanistan.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But like other former officials, Mr. Jones said that ramping up the operations of the militias while drawing down the American military would be impractical and ineffective.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">A peace deal that pulls out American forces but does not disarm the Taliban would give it control of larger parts of Afghanistan, effectively creating a safe haven for terrorist groups that no increase in C.I.A. support to the militias could counter, Mr. Jones warned.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">C.I.A.-supported militias operate across Afghanistan and are used by the United States and the Afghan government to target terrorist and insurgent cells.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">These militias have taken on increasingly dangerous missions in Afghanistan in the past year, seeking out hard-to-find and well-defended terrorist leaders, a former senior Defense Department official said.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militas4/merlin_137904945_67fa4298-fef2-4ac7-b90a-39a1ac6d16f2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">The C.I.A. director, Gina Haspel, has highlighted logistical problems with expanding the agency’s work in Afghanistan.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Tom Brenner/The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">They trace their roots to the immediate aftermath of the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks, when the C.I.A. began assembling a patchwork alliance of warlord-led fighting groups to topple the Taliban and pursue Qaeda fighters.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">After the fall of the Taliban and the establishment of a new Afghan government, the C.I.A.’s shadowy paramilitary arm, known as Ground Branch, began transforming the fighting groups. Some developed into large, well-trained and equipped militias that initially worked outside the auspices of the Afghan government. The militias were used for sensitive and covert missions, including pursuing terrorist leaders across the border into Pakistan’s lawless frontier territory.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In more recent years, the agency’s hold over militant groups and other regional counterterrorism forces and strike teams has waned some, former officials said. Many of the militias now fall under the command of Afghanistan’s own intelligence service, the National Directorate of Security. But there is little doubt they are still advised, and often directed, by the C.I.A.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Taliban’s disdain for the C.I.A.’s Afghan counterpart has been apparent in recent months. In July, a bomb targeting the Afghan covert service <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2019/07/afghanistan-killed-taliban-car-bomb-attack-ghazni-190707060218685.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">killed eight members</a> and six civilians, and wounded hundreds more. In January, Taliban fighters infiltrated an Afghan intelligence base in Wardak Province, killing dozens in one of the deadliest attacks on the service during the nearly 18-year war.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Fighting in Afghanistan has increased since peace discussions began as both sides try to strengthen their positions. Taliban fighters mounted <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/01/world/asia/afghanistan-taliban-peace-talks.html?module=inline" title="">two attacks</a> over the weekend, including <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/08/31/world/asia/taliban-attack-kunduz-afghanistan.html?module=inline" title="">one in the northern city of Kunduz</a> that killed the top police spokesman and wounded the police chief, according to local officials.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In a <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://radio.foxnews.com/2019/08/29/president-trump-talks-china-trade-afghanistan-and-fox-news-with-brian-kilmeade-on-fox-news-radio/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Fox News interview</a> last week, President Trump alluded to keeping American forces, and perhaps the C.I.A., in Afghanistan after any deal with the Taliban is reached. “We are reducing that presence very substantially and we’re going to always have a presence and we’re going to have high intelligence,” he said.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Trump said that the troop level in the country would be reduced to 8,600, down from roughly 14,000. The military has pushed a plan to gradually draw down forces, but administration officials have fiercely debated the precise timeline.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The president has been vague about his preferred outcome on the current peace proposal or the plan to expand the C.I.A. role, and Ms. Haspel has also withheld her opinion in meetings. Secretary of State Mike Pompeo has been driving the peace negotiations forward. John R. Bolton, the national security adviser, opposes the current peace deal, largely on worries about whether Afghanistan can keep terrorists at bay on its own.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/09/01/us/politics/01dc-militias/01dc-militias-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:202.35555555555558px"></div></div></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">Afghan security forces inspecting the site of a suicide attack in 2016 on the National Directorate of Security.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Haroon Sabawoon/Anadolu Agency, via Getty Images</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Senior military leaders are divided. Some believe the peace talks are worth trying, but many remain worried that a troop drawdown that moves too quickly will lead to a collapse of the country.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Increasing the C.I.A.’s role in Afghanistan as troop numbers decrease is not a new idea. In 2014, as the Obama administration considered withdrawing all American troops from the country by 2016, policymakers weighed using the agency-sponsored militias as an Afghan counterterrorism force.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But the C.I.A.-backed militias are deeply controversial within the wider Afghan population. <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/31/world/asia/cia-afghanistan-strike-force.html?module=inline" title="">Afghans have charged</a> that they are responsible for attacks that left many civilians dead and use brutal tactics that have turned large swaths of Afghans against the forces. Last month, tribal elders said that a raid by the Afghan-intelligence-backed forces <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.tolonews.com/index.php/afghanistan/11-civilians-killed-special-unit%E2%80%99s-raid-paktia-residents" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">killed 11 civilians in Paktia Province</a>, prompting the Afghan government to begin investigating.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">While the C.I.A.’s precise footprint in Afghanistan is unclear, the agency invested more resources into the country at the start of the Trump administration in an effort to <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/10/22/world/asia/cia-expanding-taliban-fight-afghanistan.html?module=inline" title="">pursue Taliban fighters</a>. Now, agency paramilitary officers — working often from an annex near the American Embassy in Kabul — team up with militias and other small Afghan intelligence teams across the country to go after Al Qaeda, the Islamic State, the Haqqani Network and often various factions within the Taliban, current and former officials said.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But small groups of the American military’s Special Operations troops also provide critical support and training for the militias. (C.I.A. teams supported by American commandos, long known as <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/10/22/world/asia/cia-expanding-taliban-fight-afghanistan.html?module=inline" title="">Omega Teams</a>, are now mostly composed of soldiers drawn from the Army’s elite Ranger regiment.)</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">For the C.I.A. militias to serve as an effective counterterrorism force, those American military teams would need to remain, even if only with a few dozen people, in different parts of the country, current and former officials said.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The exact size and nature of the agency’s presence in Afghanistan are closely guarded secrets, and details about the militia groups the C.I.A. advises are also murky.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Even with continued military support, expanding the agency’s work would mean extending one of the deadliest missions in the agency’s history.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">At least 20 C.I.A. members have been killed in Afghanistan during the war, according to current and former officials. In July, an Army bomb disposal technician was severely wounded during a C.I.A.-led mission, and an agency contractor was killed over Memorial Day weekend.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div></section><div class="bottom-of-article"><div class="css-1ubp8k9"></div><div class="css-1yif149"><p>Adam Goldman contributed reporting.</p> </div><div 2.6-2.8s-1.5-2.4-3.1-2.4c-.8 0-2.5.3-3.7.5-1.4.3-2.8.5-3.2.5-.7 0-1.5-.3-1.5-1.3 0-.8.7-1.5 2.4-1.5.9 0 2 .1 3.1.4 1.2.3 2.3.6 3.3.6 1.5 0 2.8-.5 2.8-2.6V3.7l1.2-1-.2-.2zm-4.1 6.1c-.3.3-.7.6-1.2.6s-1-.3-1.2-.6V4.2l1-.7 1.4 1.3v3.8zm0 3c-.2-.2-.7-.5-1.2-.5s-1 .3-1.2.5V9c.2.2.7.5 1.2.5s1-.3 1.2-.5v2.6zm0 4.7c0 .8-.5 1.6-1.6 1.6h-.8V12c.2-.2.7-.5 1.2-.5s.9.3 1.2.5v4.3zm13.7-7.1l-3.2-2.3-4.9 2.8v6.5l-1 .8.1.2.8-.6 3.2 2.4 5-3V9.2zm-5.4 6.3V8.3l2.5 1.8v7.1l-2.5-1.7zm14.9-8.4h-.2c-.3.2-.6.4-.9.4-.4 0-.9-.2-1.1-.5h-.2l-1.7 1.9-1.7-1.9-3 2 .1.2.8-.5 1 1.1v6.3l-1.3 1 .2.2.6-.5 2.4 2 3.1-2.1-.1-.2-.9.5-1.2-1V9c.5.5 1.1 1 1.8 1 1.4.1 2.2-1.3 2.3-2.9zm12 9.6L123 19l-4.6-7 3.3-5.1h.2c.4.4 1 .8 1.7.8s1.2-.4 1.5-.8h.2c-.1 2-1.5 3.2-2.5 3.2s-1.5-.5-2.1-.8l-.3.5 5 7.4 1-.6v.1zm-11-.5l-1.3 1 .2.2.6-.5 2.2 2 3-2-.2-.2-.8.5-1-1V.8h-.1l-3.6 2.4v.2c.4.2 1 .3 1 1.5v11.3zM143 2.9c0-2-1.9-2.5-3.4-2.5v.3c.9 0 1.6.3 1.6 1 0 .4-.3 1-1.2 1-.7 0-2.2-.4-3.3-.8-1.3-.4-2.5-.8-3.5-.8-2 0-3.4 1.5-3.4 3.2 0 1.5 1.1 2 1.5 2.2l.1-.2c-.3-.2-.6-.4-.6-1 0-.4.4-1.1 1.4-1.1.9 0 2.1.4 3.7.9 1.4.4 2.9.7 3.7.8V9l-1.5 1.3v.1l1.5 1.3V16c-.8.5-1.7.6-2.5.6-1.5 0-2.8-.4-3.9-1.6l4.1-2V6l-5 2.2c.5-1.3 1.6-2.2 2.6-2.9l-.1-.2c-3 .8-5.7 3.5-5.7 6.9 0 4 3.3 7 7 7 4 0 6.6-3.2 6.6-6.5h-.2c-.6 1.3-1.5 2.5-2.6 3.1v-4.1l1.6-1.3v-.1L140 8.8v-3c1.5 0 3-1 3-2.9zm-8.7 11l-1.2.6c-.7-.9-1.1-2.1-1.1-3.8 0-.7.1-1.5.3-2.1l2.1-.9-.1 6.2zm12.2-12h-.1l-2 1.7v.1l1.7 1.9h.2l2-1.7v-.1l-1.8-1.9zm3 14.8l-.8.5-1-1V9.3l1-.7-.2-.2-.7.6-1.8-2.1-2.9 2 .2.3.7-.5.9 1.1v6.5l-1.3 1
